Surveillance would record this in the smallest behaviors — the way she sets a cup down without sound, the way she pauses at thresholds, the way her head tilts a quarter-inch toward any door that opens behind her. She has, since adolescence, lived in **listening posture**.

The second governing principle is **portable life**. She has moved residences more than two dozen times across canon: Desert Base housing as a child, Gamma Base quarters, married quarters with Talbot, university-adjacent rentals during graduate work, Pantheon-era Mount housing, the Florida hospice where she nearly died, post-resurrection Intelligencia safehouses, the apartment she keeps as Red She-Hulk. She owns almost nothing. The objects she keeps across moves are minimal and consistent: a wooden hairbrush that was her mother's, a small carved wooden horse (Karen Ross's), a Tiffany-blue cardigan from undergrad, a single photograph of her mother in a silver frame, a worn copy of *Middlemarch*, and — across most eras — a notebook. The notebook is replaced when full; the contents are not preserved. She does not keep journals across years. Each notebook is for the woman she currently is.

---

## II. THE PRE-DAWN BLOCK (04:30–06:30)

She wakes early and has done so since childhood, when her father held household reveille at 05:00. The waking is **mechanical, not refreshed** — she does not stretch luxuriously, she does not linger. Pre-Red, she rose without an alarm at 05:15–05:45 depending on era; post-Red, when transformation-state metabolism is unpredictable, she has used a phone alarm set for 05:00 that she silences within one ring.

Surveillance would log the following invariants:
- **Bed-making before bathroom.** Hospital corners, executed in under ninety seconds, a holdover from Thunderbolt. She has never not made her bed. Even on the day of her miscarriage, the hospital nurse would later remark that she had made it before going to the bathroom that began the bleeding.
- **Bathroom routine: 11–14 minutes.** Shower hot to the threshold of pain; she has remarked across runs that she likes the bathroom mirror fogged so she does not have to look at her own face first thing. No music. She brushes her teeth in the shower to save time, a habit from graduate school under deadline.
- **Hair: pulled back wet.** She has worn variations of the same low practical knot since age seventeen. The mother's wooden brush is used last, after the elastic; ritual rather than function.
- **No breakfast at home.** She drinks coffee — black, no sugar, since residency-style scientific training. Pre-Red, one mug. Post-Red, three. The coffee is made the night before in a stovetop moka pot (her preference since a postdoctoral year that surveillance would trace to the David run); she reheats it rather than re-brewing. This is a small economy of effort that recurs across her life: **she will reheat anything rather than start over**.

The pre-dawn block ends, in most eras, with her standing at a window. This is one of her most consistent micro-rituals across forty years of canon: a stationary two-to-five-minute window-stand, coffee in left hand, right hand at her elbow, looking at nothing in particular. It is the only time of day she is reliably not in motion. Field operatives would describe it as **a vigil for no specified arrival**.

---

## III. THE DAY-WORK BLOCK

### As Bruce's partner / Gamma Base researcher (Silver/Bronze Age through David era)

Her professional life, when permitted by writers, is **cellular biology and biophysics**, with sub-specialization in radiation biology — a discipline she chose, transparently, to understand what happened to Bruce. She is competent. The MCU iteration's foregrounding of this is consistent with the comics' undercurrent. Her work hours, when she has them, are 07:30–17:30 nominal, frequently extending into the late evening when she is alone. She is the kind of researcher who works through lunch and then forgets she did.

Surveillance of her work behavior would record:
- **Lab posture: hunched, focused, low affect.** She does not chat. She greets colleagues by name once at first contact in the morning and then minimally for the rest of the day.
- **Notebooks kept in pencil**, dated, with margin notes in a tighter second hand. She does not trust digital lab notebooks alone; redundancy is a survival reflex.
- **One coffee per hour after 10:00.** She does not eat lunch on lab days unless someone places food in front of her. When someone does — and Marlo Chandler frequently did — she eats it.
- **Music when alone in the lab:** classical, low volume, Bach preferred (the *Goldberg Variations* and the cello suites recur). She does not play music she has emotional history with; she keeps her music neutral. This is deliberate compartmentalization.
- **Calls from her father go unanswered in the moment and returned within four hours.** Calls from Bruce are answered on the first ring regardless of context, a fact colleagues across multiple workplaces have noted and gently teased her about. She does not laugh when they tease her about this.

She leaves the lab before dark when Bruce is present in her life and stays until midnight when he is not. The pattern is so reliable it could be used to triangulate Bruce's location.

### As Red She-Hulk / post-resurrection

Work life largely absent. Days are unstructured, which she does not enjoy and will not admit. She fills them with physical training (running, weight work, contact sparring at gyms she joins under aliases and quits within months) and with reading — she retains the biophysicist's reading habits even without the lab. Surveillance would log her at three or four bookstores in any given city, always used. She does not order books online; she likes the **walk to and from**.

Without an external schedule she fabricates one: she runs at the same time daily, eats at the same time daily, drinks at the same time daily. The discipline is real and is doing the work that the lab schedule used to do. When the discipline collapses — and it has, several times — she does not eat for thirty-six hours, drinks heavily, and sleeps in her clothes.

---

## IV. INTERSTITIAL HABITS, TICS & MICRO-PATTERNS

A surveillance team's interstitial log — the noise between the headlines — would reveal Betty more honestly than any of her stated routines.

- **The thumb-ring rotation.** She wore a wedding band during the marriage to Bruce. After her death and resurrection, she wears a plain silver band on her right thumb, and rotates it three times with her index finger when stressed. Frequency increases sharply in proximity to either her father or Bruce.
- **The pause at thresholds.** Half a second, three-quarters at most. She is checking the room before entering. She is unaware she does it. She has done it since she was twelve.
- **The four-cigarette window.** During the Talbot marriage and during the year before her death, she smoked — four cigarettes a day, never more, always at the same intervals (after morning coffee, after lunch, after the workday, before bed). The control is the point; the cigarettes are almost incidental. She quit twice and resumed twice. Post-resurrection she does not smoke; the alcohol has replaced this function.
- **The phone face-down.** Across every era in which phones exist, she keeps hers face-down. Not from secrecy — from a refusal to be summoned visually. She will answer; she will not be glanced at.
- **The two-bite rule.** When upset, she will take two bites of any meal placed in front of her and then push it aside. A friend (Marlo, once) counted it across a week.
- **The neck-touch.** Right hand to the side of her neck, fingertips against the carotid. Self-soothing. Frequency correlates almost perfectly with mentions of her mother, Bruce in danger, or her father's voice.
- **Walking pace.** Brisk and even on sidewalks, deliberately slow indoors. The brisk pace is military daughter; the slow indoor pace is to give the room time to react to her presence rather than be surprised by it.
- **The left-hand seat.** She chooses seats that put her left side to the wall and her right side to the room, every time, in any restaurant. Right hand free. It is an old habit and she does not know where it came from. (Surveillance speculation: childhood meals with Thunderbolt, who sat to her right.)
- **She does not photograph anything.** Across decades. She does not take pictures of meals, places, herself, others. She has been photographed (Gamma Base PR, wedding, news coverage of her death and return) but does not photograph. This is consistent with a person who does not trust the future to want the past.

---

## V. EVENINGS

### Pre-Red

Evenings were structured around **return** — Bruce's return from a lab, a mission, a fugue, a country. When he was present, dinner was at 19:00, cooked by her (simple, repetitive: roasted chicken, pasta, vegetables, the same six dishes in rotation; she is a competent and unadventurous cook). They ate at a small table, often in silence that she experienced as companionship and he experienced as relief. She drank one glass of white wine. He did not drink.

After dinner: reading on the same couch, her feet under his thigh. This was, by her own admission to Marlo, the happiest part of her life. The reading was real — novels, biographies of women scientists, occasionally poetry (Bishop, Levertov). She is not a critic; she reads to be elsewhere.

When Bruce was absent — which was most evenings of most years — she ate standing at the counter, listened to the radio (NPR, the BBC World Service later), graded papers or processed lab data, and went to bed by 22:30. **She kept the second pillow on the bed regardless.** A nurse during her hospitalization observed it; her father did not.

### Post-Red

Evenings are the danger zone. The discipline that holds her days together does not extend past sundown. Surveillance would log the following pattern across most weeks:

- 18:00–19:30: a run, hard, hood up.
- 19:30–20:30: shower, change, leave the apartment.
- 20:30–01:30: bar. Often the same bar for weeks at a stretch, then never that bar again. She sits at the end of the bar, left side to the wall. She drinks whiskey neat, then beer, then whiskey again. She does not chat with the bartender beyond ordering. She watches the door.
- She leaves alone roughly three nights of five. Of the remaining two, she leaves with company perhaps half the time — sometimes a man, occasionally a woman, never the same one twice. Surveillance would note that the encounters appear to be **about not being looked at the way Bruce looked at her** — i.e., the partner is selected for being maximally not-Bruce.
- She does not bring people to her own residence. Hotel rooms or theirs.
- She returns home alone, always, before sunrise. The pre-dawn block holds even when the evening has not.

### The bathtub

A constant across eras: she takes long baths. Pre-Red, two or three a week, hot to the threshold; post-Red, almost nightly, with the lights off. She reads in the bath. She has ruined many books. She does not consider this a loss; the reading was the point and the book was the medium.

---

## VI. WEEKLY & MONTHLY PATTERNS

A week of surveillance would record:

- **Monday–Friday:** the day-work structure described above. Predictability within ± fifteen minutes on most variables.
- **Saturday morning:** the only morning she sleeps past 07:00, and only by an hour. She does laundry on Saturday. She has done laundry on Saturday since she was eleven, when her mother died and the household chore fell to her by default. The folding is precise and fast. She does not iron unless she must.
- **Saturday afternoon:** errands, library or bookstore, occasionally a film alone (matinees; she dislikes evening crowds). Pre-Red, she sometimes met Marlo for lunch on Saturday. She has not replaced Marlo since.
- **Saturday night:** historically quiet. Post-Red, the most volatile night of the week.
- **Sunday:** the hardest day. No work to defer to. She fills it with cleaning the apartment to a standard exceeding any reasonable need, with grocery shopping (a single trip, list in hand, executed in under forty minutes), with long phone calls she does not make and emails she does not send. She has spent a great many Sundays standing in her own kitchen with her hand on the counter, doing nothing.

Monthly:
- **First-of-the-month:** she pays bills in a single sitting, immediately on receipt. She has never paid a bill late. The control is congruent with everything else.
- **Mid-month:** a longer-distance run or hike, alone. She likes ridgelines and dry desert. She has returned to the New Mexico desert repeatedly across her life, including post-resurrection. The desert is, to her, **the place where the worst thing happened and she did not die from it** — at least not for a long time. It steadies her.
- **Her mother's birthday and the anniversary of Karen's death:** she does not mark them outwardly. She takes a longer bath. She rereads a particular passage in *Middlemarch*. She does not cry.
- **Her own death-anniversary:** she will not name it. She drinks alone that night and the next.

---

## VII. SLEEP

Sleep is poor and always has been. Across canon she is depicted with under-eye shadowing that art teams have rendered consistently from the David run forward.

- **Sleep onset:** slow. She lies awake for forty to ninety minutes most nights. She does not use sleep medication regularly (suspected occasional benzodiazepine use during the David era; no on-page evidence of dependence). She does not read in bed — bed is for trying to sleep, and the discipline is enforced.
- **Sleep posture:** on her side, facing the door. Right hand under the pillow. Even when sleeping with a partner, she does not face them; she faces the door. Bruce, who knew this, did not take it personally.
- **Wakings:** 02:00–04:00 wakings frequent. She does not get up. She lies still and waits to fall back asleep, which she usually does. She has trained herself not to check the time. Checking the time, she once told a colleague, ruins what remains of the night.
- **Dreams:** the desert recurs. The hospital recurs. Her mother does not appear in her dreams, which she has remarked on, in writing, in one of the notebooks that was later thrown away.
- **Nightmares followed by silent sit-up at the edge of the bed, both feet flat on the floor, hands on knees, for two to five minutes.** Then she lies back down. She does not turn on the light.

---

## VIII. EATING, DRINKING & THE BODY

Eating is utilitarian. She does not cook for herself with care; she cooks for others and eats their leftovers. Her own food is plain — yogurt, toast, eggs, salad, rice with vegetables. She has a sweet tooth she does not indulge in front of others; in private she keeps a small bar of good dark chocolate and takes one square in the evening. She has done this since graduate school.

Alcohol pre-Red: one glass of wine with dinner, sometimes two, almost never three.

Alcohol post-Red: substantial. Whiskey is preferred. She drinks faster than the company she is in, finishes first, orders next. Her transformation-state physiology metabolizes alcohol unevenly, which has meant on several occasions she has been drunker than she expected to be, and on others not drunk at all when she wanted to be. The unpredictability is part of why she drinks alone often: she does not want witnesses to the inconsistency.

She does not exercise for vanity. She exercises because **her body has been done things to**, and the running and the lifting are a reclaiming of motion she chose. She does not own a scale. She owns one full-length mirror and uses it briefly.

---

## IX. WHAT A WEEK OF SURVEILLANCE WOULD ACTUALLY LOOK LIKE

Compressed log, representative week, post-resurrection / Red-capable era. Pre-Red weeks would substitute lab and Bruce-vigil structures for the evening blocks but retain the same morning architecture.

- **Monday.** 05:00 wake. 05:15 shower. 05:35 coffee, window-stand, four minutes. 06:00 out the door. Walk (not drive) to a café 0.7 miles away; second coffee; reads at the counter, twenty minutes. 06:45 to gym; lifts heavy for fifty minutes; minimal interaction. 08:00 home; change. 09:00–17:00 unstructured — reading, errands, walks. Phone face-down throughout. One call answered (unknown; brief). 18:00 run, 8 km, hard. 19:30 dinner at home, standing: yogurt, toast, an apple. 20:30 bath, lights off, ninety minutes. 22:30 bed. 23:40 asleep. 03:10 waking; lies still; back asleep by 03:45.

- **Tuesday.** Same wake structure. Daytime: long bookstore walk, two purchases (a biography, a novel). Lunch with no one, eaten while walking. Afternoon at a public library, working through a stack. 18:00 run. 20:00 leaves apartment. Bar from 20:30 to 00:45. Three whiskeys, two beers. Speaks to no one beyond the bartender. Leaves alone. Home 01:15. In bed 01:45. Pre-dawn block delayed; coffee at 06:20 on Wednesday.

- **Wednesday.** Slightly degraded version of Monday. The pre-dawn window-stand is longer — eleven minutes. She is thinking about something. She does not write it down.

- **Thursday.** Long-distance drive out of the city to a trailhead. Six-hour hike, alone, no music. Returns at dusk. Eats a real meal — pasta, wine, at home, sitting down. Reads in the bath until the water cools twice (she runs more hot in). Sleeps well; no waking. This is her best day of most weeks.

- **Friday.** Restless. Cleans the apartment in the afternoon. Two phone calls considered and not made (one to her father; one to a number she will not name). Out by 20:00. Bar; encounter; leaves with company at 23:30. Hotel. Returns to her apartment at 04:50, in time for the pre-dawn block, which she executes on schedule.

- **Saturday.** Laundry. Errands. Library. Afternoon film (alone). Quiet evening at home, a single glass of whiskey, a novel, an early bed.

- **Sunday.** The hardest day. Long bath in the morning. Stands at the kitchen counter for approximately forty minutes around midday, doing nothing observable. Goes for a long walk. Cooks a real meal she does not finish. Lies on the couch reading until 21:00. In bed by 22:00. Does not fall asleep until past 01:00. The window-stand on Monday morning will be six minutes instead of four.

---

## X. SYNTHESIS

What surveillance would ultimately record is a woman whose entire daily architecture is built to **hold** something — first her father's volatility, then her own grief, then Bruce's dissociation, then her own resurrected rage. The routines are not preferences; they are scaffolding. Every consistent habit — the bed made in ninety seconds, the phone face-down, the left-hand seat, the bath in the dark, the window-stand at dawn — is a small act of containment performed by a person whose body has repeatedly failed to contain what was done to it.

The pattern reveals what the diagnostic frame already specified: she has not yet learned to **want** her own days. She has learned, beautifully and exhaustively, to **survive** them. The open question — the one any longitudinal file on her will eventually have to answer — is whether, given enough uninterrupted weeks, she will begin to build a life she has chosen, or whether she will simply keep refining the architecture of waiting.

Current assessment: the architecture is still the architecture of waiting. But the window-stand is getting shorter, year over year. That is something.


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# RELATIONSHIPS I — FAMILY & ORIGIN BONDS

*Subject: Elizabeth "Betty" Ross. Family-of-origin and blood-tier mapping. Dyadic depth, longitudinal. All inferences substantiated against cited record.*

---

## 1. GENERAL THADDEUS E. "THUNDERBOLT" ROSS — Father

### History of the Bond

The defining relationship of the subject's life, longer and deeper than any romantic attachment, and the template that all subsequent attachments are organized against. Thunderbolt Ross is a career Army officer of an older generation — West Point, Korea, missile command, Air Cavalry mythology — who married Karen Lee Ross in the late 1940s/early 1950s and produced one child, Elizabeth, somewhere in the late 1950s/early 1960s by published-record arithmetic (Marvel sliding-timescale notwithstanding). Subject's earliest documented environments are base housing at successive Army posts, terminating in the family relocation to Desert Base, New Mexico, where Ross commanded the gamma-bomb project that initiates *Incredible Hulk* #1 (1962).

From the earliest established memories (recounted in *Hulk: Gray*, Loeb/Sale, 2003, and David's flashback sequences in *Hulk* vol. 1 #347–351 and the Pantheon arc), the father–daughter dynamic is structured around three persistent vectors:

1. **Command displacement.** Ross addresses Betty as a subordinate, even in domestic settings. Diction is imperative; affection is performative and rare; refusals are framed as insubordination rather than disagreement. Subject learned by age six that "no" had to be inflected as compliance or it would be overridden.
2. **Conditional approval predicated on military legibility.** Ross approved of Glenn Talbot (uniform, rank, deference) and disapproved of Banner (civilian, intellectual, physically slight, suspected of cowardice). The approval matrix had nothing to do with the men's treatment of his daughter; it had to do with their legibility to *him*.
3. **Grief refusal.** Karen Ross's death (see §3 below) constituted, for Thunderbolt, an event to be metabolized through work and silence. Betty was not permitted to mourn aloud in his presence; the household became an extension of the parade ground.

### Dynamics

The operating dyadic mode across six decades of publication is **defiance-within-orbit**. Subject has never *left* her father in any meaningful sense — geographically (Desert Base → Gamma Base → Hulkbuster Base; she follows the post), institutionally (always inside the Army's penumbra), or psychically (every major life choice is calibrated against his probable reaction). She defies, but she defies *in his sightline*. Choosing Banner is the master defiance — a man Ross hates, kept close enough for Ross to see. Marrying Banner (*Hulk* #319, 1986) is the act repeated as ritual. Even her death (*Hulk* #468, 1998) leaves her in her father's arms, not Bruce's.

Ross's affect toward her oscillates across the run between **possessive tenderness** (rare, usually under threat — kidnappings, illness, the deathbed) and **operational instrumentalization** (he uses her as bait, as leverage on Banner, and ultimately as raw material — sanctioning or directly enabling her Red She-Hulk transformation in Loeb's *Hulk* vol. 2 alongside the Intelligencia/Leader plotline). The Loeb arc is the pivot at which his pattern of bodily disposal of her becomes undeniable text rather than subtext: he authorizes the reconstitution of her body for tactical purposes and frames it as paternal love.

Subject's reciprocal mode is, until 2008, **silent endurance laced with strategic disobedience**. Post-resurrection, the silent half collapses: Red She-Hulk confronts him directly (Loeb, Aaron), strikes him, and articulates for the first time the accusation that has been latent since 1962 — *you used me*. The articulation does not produce reconciliation; it produces standoff. They remain in orbit.

### Evolution

- **1962–1970 (Lee/Kirby/Trimpe):** Pure patriarchal command structure. Betty is "Ross's daughter," named relationally even by the narration. Her interiority toward him is implied through facial reaction shots — pained compliance.
- **1970–1986 (Mantlo and transitional):** The Talbot marriage is engineered partly by his pressure; its collapse is partly his fault (he uses Talbot against Banner with disregard for his son-in-law's welfare). Betty begins, very faintly, to resent.
- **1986–1998 (David):** First sustained on-page confrontations. She names his coldness about Karen. She defends Banner to his face. She still, however, returns to him at every crisis. The marriage to Bruce humiliates Ross; the miscarriage gives him grim satisfaction he is too disciplined to express but Betty registers it.
- **1998 (death):** Ross's grief is total but expressed in the only register he owns — vengeance against Banner. He blames Bruce at her bedside while she is still conscious. Subject dies hearing her father weaponize her death against the man she chose.
- **2008–present (Loeb, Aaron, *Defenders*):** Resurrection. The Red She-Hulk era is the first time the daughter has more physical power than the father. Confrontations become bodily. Ross — also now Red Hulk — and Betty exist as parallel transformed beings, a literalization of the Ross family disease: *we become monsters and call it duty*. There are moments of strained alliance (joint Hulk operations, *Hulk* vol. 2 late issues) and moments of open violence. The bond is never severed; it is never repaired.

### Trust and Safety

Trust profile is **catastrophically asymmetric**. Subject trusts Ross with her physical safety in any combat scenario — he will move heaven and earth to retrieve her body; he has done so repeatedly. She does not trust him with her body's *integrity* (he authorized its weaponization), with her emotional disclosures (he weaponizes those too), with any partner she chooses, or with the truth of her interior life. Safety in his presence is the safety of a fortified compound under the command of an unstable governor: the perimeter holds, but inside it she is surveilled and revisable.

Ross's trust in her is more complicated than he allows: he distrusts her judgment on men, distrusts her loyalty when Banner is in play, and yet relies on her absolute discretion regarding base classified material — she has been read into more black-budget Army programs than any non-uniformed civilian in her cohort and has never leaked.

### Failure Mode

The dyad fails predictably along two axes:

1. **When Bruce appears.** Any presence, communication, or rumor of Banner reverts both parties to their 1962 positions. Ross becomes the General; Betty becomes the daughter who must be controlled and who must defy. Decades of partial growth evaporate inside ten panels.
2. **When grief is in the room.** Neither can metabolize loss in the other's presence. Karen's death set the protocol; the miscarriage reactivated it; Betty's own death and resurrection have not resolved it. They cannot cry together. The closest they come is the deathbed scene in *Hulk* #468, and even there Ross converts the grief into rage before it can crystallize as mourning.

### Leverage

The General has lifelong, multi-axis leverage on subject: institutional (security clearances, base access), informational (knows her medical history in detail, knows her finances, knows her drinking pattern post-resurrection), affective (her unappeased desire for his approval remains operative even when she denies it), and existential (he literally authorized the reconstitution of her body; he can credibly imply he could do or undo it again). Counter-leverage is real but narrower: she is the only person on Earth whose disapproval registers with him as wound rather than nuisance. She knows the precise wording that breaks him — the invocation of her mother's name in his hearing — and she has used it sparingly, perhaps three times across the published record, each time decisive.

---

## 2. KAREN LEE ROSS — Mother (deceased)

### History of the Bond

The mother is the most important *absent* presence in the file. Established across the David run and explicitly textured in *Hulk: Gray* and scattered flashbacks (*Hulk* vol. 1 #325, #347; *Red She-Hulk* tie-ins), Karen Ross is depicted as a warm, literate, somewhat melancholic Army wife who structured the household's emotional life entirely — because Thunderbolt did not. She is the source of subject's literacy, her love of horses (Betty's adolescent equestrianism, referenced in David and Loeb), her scientific curiosity (mother encouraged the inquiries the father dismissed), and her capacity for tenderness.

Karen died when Betty was an adolescent — variously implied to be early- to mid-teens — of a wasting illness (cancer in most readings, never specified by treatment regimen on-page). The illness was prolonged enough for Betty to function as caretaker in its final stages; Thunderbolt was largely absent on deployment or, when present, emotionally walled.

### Dynamics

The living relationship, by inference from all accounts, was the only **secure-base attachment** of subject's life. Karen was the mirror in which Betty first saw herself as a person rather than as Ross's daughter. The dyad provided: validation of emotion, encouragement of curiosity, modeling of female competence inside a constraining domestic structure, and — crucially — the experience of being *known* without being managed.

The dying relationship installed a pattern that would govern subject's adult life: **love expressed as vigil at the bedside of a body that is failing**. Subject sat with her mother through the dying. She would, decades later, sit with Bruce through dissociative collapses, with Talbot through PTSD, and would herself die slowly in a bed while loved ones watched. The Karen-vigil is the somatic template for how subject *does* love.

### Evolution

In life: the mother was Betty's home. In death: the mother became the absence that defines subject's interior architecture. Across the publication record, subject does not speak of Karen often, but the references are diagnostically dense:

- A photograph kept in her quarters at Hulkbuster Base (David era, background detail).
- The single occasion (*Hulk* vol. 1 #340-range) she invokes her mother to Bruce, framing her capacity to tolerate his condition as something she "learned watching someone else fade."
- The choice, after marrying Bruce, to keep her own surname — Ross — which is also her mother's married name. Subject has never been a "Mrs. Banner" on the page.
- Post-resurrection, in moments of self-confrontation, the rare admission that she does not know what her mother would think of who she has become. This is the question she cannot resolve.

The grief was never permitted full course. Thunderbolt did not allow tears; the funeral was clipped; mourning rituals were truncated. Subject carries unprocessed loss of the foundational tier — what therapeutic literature would call **disenfranchised grief** — and it has shaped every loss since (Talbot's first death, the miscarriage, her own dying) as a re-encounter with the same un-mourned event.

### Trust and Safety

Karen was the only person in subject's life who was both fully trustworthy and fully safe. The bond is the calibration standard against which all other intimacy is unconsciously measured and found wanting. This is part of the trap: no living person can pass the test of a perfectly-remembered, idealized dead mother whose love was uncomplicated because it ended before complication.

### Failure Mode

The bond cannot fail; it ended. But the *idealization* fails subject continuously. She compares Bruce's intermittent presence to her mother's reliable warmth and feels the shortfall; she compares her father's coldness to her mother's heat and the household breaks; she compares her own choices to what she imagines her mother would have chosen and finds, especially post-resurrection, a verdict she cannot stand to hear. The mother is the standard she cannot meet and cannot stop measuring against.

### Leverage

The dead have only the leverage the living grant them. Karen's leverage on subject is total and entirely self-administered. Anyone who knows about Karen — Thunderbolt, Bruce in their most intimate moments, possibly Marlo Chandler — can collapse subject's defenses by invoking her. The General has done this perhaps twice in the record, and regretted it. Bruce has invoked her once or twice, gently, and it has been the only register in which subject has fully wept in his presence on the page.

---

## 3. BRUCE BANNER — Husband (chosen family, biologically entangled)

*Treated here as family because subject married him, lost a pregnancy by him, was killed by his nemesis as a proxy for him, and remains bound to him at the level of cellular biology. Romantic-dyad analysis sits elsewhere in this file; this entry covers the kinship-tier dimensions only.*

### History of the Bond (Kinship Vector)

Met at Desert Base in the run-up to the gamma test. Engaged informally for years; engagement broken and reformed multiple times across the Lee/Trimpe/Mantlo runs; formally married in *Hulk* #319 (1986) in a ceremony attended by a thin, strained guest list — Rick Jones, a few colleagues, no functional family beyond Thunderbolt's grudging absence/presence depending on the reading. The marriage is, in its kinship aspect, the act by which subject formally constituted a family-of-choice in opposition to her family-of-origin.

The kinship dimension acquired biological reality in the **pregnancy and miscarriage** (David, Pantheon arc, *Hulk* vol. 1 mid-#360s through #400s implications). Gamma-irradiated reproductive cells produced a non-viable pregnancy. The lost child constitutes a phantom family member — a presence-by-absence that joins Karen in subject's interior landscape of un-mourned dead.

### Dynamics

Within the kinship frame, Bruce occupies the role of **the husband she chose to make her family with, who could not give her one**. The miscarriage is the spinal trauma of this dyad as kin. Subject does not, on the page, openly blame Bruce. But the displacement is visible: she became, after the loss, more invested in caring for *him* (which is endless and substitutive work) and less invested in any future for them as a household. The marriage continues; the household never materializes. They live in safe houses, on the run, in base quarters, in the Pantheon's Mount. There is no home.

### Evolution

- **Engagement years (1962–1986):** Family-by-promise. Always deferred.
- **Marriage to death (1986–1998):** Family-by-vow, undermined by the inability to make family-by-body. Subject's role calcifies into wife-as-caregiver, the role her father refused to play for her mother. She becomes, structurally, both Karen and the daughter who tended Karen — collapsed into the same person.
- **Death and resurrection (1998–2008+):** The marriage is, in legal-narrative terms, suspended by her death and never formally resumed. Resurrected, she does not return to him as wife. The Red She-Hulk era treats Bruce as ex, antagonist, and occasional lover-of-convenience. The kinship bond is *not* renounced — she still uses Ross, not Banner, as her surname, but she also does not divorce — it is unresolved.

### Trust and Safety

Subject trusts Bruce with her interior life more than with her body. She trusts him not to hurt her on purpose; she does not trust the Other to make the same choice. This is the foundational paradox of the marriage as kinship: she chose, as her family, a man who comes with a member of the household she did not invite and cannot evict.

### Failure Mode

The dyad fails reproductively (the miscarriage), domestically (no stable home), and protectively (her death). It does not fail as bond — she returns to him in some register across every era, even in hostility.

### Leverage

Bruce holds, even now, the asymmetric leverage that her primary attachment was to him; he can collapse her composure with a single sustained look. Subject holds the reciprocal leverage that she is the only person on Earth Banner believes ever fully saw him. Neither has ever used this leverage maliciously. Both have used it accidentally, with damage.

---

## 4. THE UNBORN CHILD — Phantom Kin

The lost pregnancy is, in any honest mapping of subject's family, an entry of its own. Marvel's editorial treatment moved on; subject did not. The child exists in her interior architecture as a third absent figure, beside Karen and beside the version of herself who died in 1998.

### Dynamics

There is no dynamic; there is only the shape of what would have been a dynamic. The shape is precise: subject would have been the mother she had — warm, attentive, literate, present — and would have *consciously* refused to be the father she had. The child would have grown up off-base, civilian, in a household with books. This is the future she imagined and lost.

### Evolution

The loss has not evolved. It has been displaced — onto care of Bruce, onto care of Marlo during her crises, onto a more reluctant care of others post-resurrection. The displacement is the evidence of the unprocessed core. Post-Red, subject's sexual openness and refusal of domesticity can be read partly as a defense against ever again wanting what she lost.

### Trust, Safety, Failure, Leverage

Not applicable in dyadic terms. As a pressure point: maximally exploitable, never to be raised lightly, and the single subject capable of producing immediate dissociative withdrawal in subject at any age or transformation state. The General knows this; Banner knows this; the Abomination knew it (and chose, instead, to kill her). The lost child is the locked room of the file.

---

## 5. EXTENDED ROSS KIN — Uncles, Cousins, Lineage

The published record is sparse here, which is itself diagnostic. Thunderbolt is presented as the terminus of a line of Ross military men (a paternal grandfather, military, alluded to in flashbacks; collateral cousins implied but never named). Karen's family-of-origin is essentially absent from canon — no maternal grandmother in the household after Karen's death, no aunts visiting, no cousins. Subject grew up effectively without an extended kin network, which compounded the foundational isolation of the nuclear unit.

The functional consequence is that subject has no "fallback" family — no aunt's house to flee to as a teenager, no cousin to call as an adult. When the Ross-Banner nuclear structure fails, there is no kin lattice to catch her. This explains, in part, the disproportionate weight she places on the few lateral bonds she has been able to form in adulthood (Marlo Chandler chief among them — covered in the next section of this file).

### Leverage

None. The Ross line's leverage on her is concentrated entirely in the General. Karen's line is so absent it functions as another disenfranchised loss — subject does not know her maternal grandparents' graves, does not maintain her mother's family's correspondence, did not inherit objects beyond a small set of personal effects (a hairbrush, a book of poetry, a photograph; referenced in passing in David's run as items kept in her base quarters).

---

## 6. GLENN TALBOT — Brother-Tier / Quasi-Kin (deceased)

*Listed under family rather than romance because the marriage was functionally a kinship-of-convenience and Talbot occupied a role closer to a brother her father had wanted than to a chosen partner. Romantic analysis sits elsewhere.*

### History of the Bond

Major (later Colonel) Glenn Talbot enters the file in the mid-1960s as Thunderbolt's hand-picked aide, suitor, and intended son-in-law. The marriage (*Hulk* #124, 1970) is the only major life decision subject made *with* her father's approval and *against* her own template. Talbot is captured in the Soviet bloc, presumed dead, returns broken, returns again altered, and is ultimately killed (*Hulk* #258, 1981, by the Hulk in a Soviet engagement).

### Dynamics

The marriage was, in subject's interior accounting, an experiment in being the daughter her father wanted. Talbot was, by most accounts, decent — earnest, dutiful, in love with her, aware that she was not in love with him. The dyad operated as a quiet, sad domesticity in which both parties knew the truth and neither named it. Subject's guilt toward Talbot is one of the persistent low-register affects of the David era; she carries his death as something she partly caused by never having fully chosen him.

### Trust, Safety, Failure, Leverage

Trust was operational and complete; safety was the safety of a man who would never hurt her and could not reach her. The failure mode was structural — the marriage was a category error from the start. Leverage was minimal in either direction; the bond's afterlife in subject's psyche functions less as leverage than as ballast — a constant low-grade reminder that her capacity for using people is real, and that her father's approval is a poison she has tasted and survived but not forgotten.

---

## 7. THE FIRST DEATH — Self as Kin (1998)

The dossier records, with reluctance, that subject must be treated as having a relationship with the version of herself who died in 1998. Resurrection without continuity-of-experience produces, clinically, an internal split between premorbid and postmortem self-states. Subject's relationship to "the woman who died in that bed" is unresolved and functions as a kinship relation to a deceased intimate.

### Dynamics

Red She-Hulk speaks of "her" — the former Betty — sometimes in third person, sometimes with contempt, occasionally with grief. The dead self is the family member subject most resembles, most resents, and most quietly mourns. The relationship structurally parallels the relationship to Karen: an idealized/contested dead woman whose standard the living subject cannot meet and cannot stop measuring against. The difference is that subject *is* this dead woman, which makes the bond inescapable.

### Failure Mode and Leverage

The split is the failure mode. Until the two selves are integrated — a process barely begun on the page — subject is structurally divided against herself, and any party (Banner, Ross, the Leader, the Intelligencia, future antagonists) who can articulate the division can exploit it. Anyone who says, in the right tone, *that isn't who you are anymore* can momentarily destabilize her in either direction.

The integration of the dead self with the living one is the unfinished family work of subject's life. It is also, by the structure of this dossier, the work no one else can do for her.

---

*End of section. Continued in Relationships II — Romantic, Sexual & Pair-Bond Trajectories; Relationships III — Lateral Bonds, Friendships & Professional Kin; Relationships IV — Adversaries, Captors & the Bodies in the Margins.*


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — ALLIES, CHOSEN FAMILY & MENTORS

*Subject: Elizabeth "Betty" Ross. Catalogue of every non-romantic, non-antagonistic significant bond across the longitudinal record. Each entry: history, dynamic, evolution, trust calibration, failure mode, points of leverage available to the subject and to others over the subject through them. Romantic bonds (Banner, Talbot, the post-Red liaisons) are handled in Relationships I; father/mother in Relationships III (family of origin). What follows is the parallel structure she has built — patchily, with long dormancies — outside the Banner–Ross gravity well.*

---

## 1. MARLO CHANDLER-JONES

**The single most important lateral bond of Betty's adult life. The control case that proves the rest of her relational pathology is partner-specific, not general.**

### History

Introduced in the Peter David run (*Incredible Hulk* #347, 1988) initially as the dance-instructor-turned-stripper love interest of Rick Jones — loud, blonde, Queens-inflected, six feet tall in heels, the structural inverse of Betty in almost every register. The two women meet in the wake of Rick's pursuit of her, when Betty is still finding her footing as Bruce's wife in the "Mr. Fixit" / Vegas / Pantheon transitional period. Initial wariness on both sides — Marlo reads Betty as the uptight scientist's-daughter brunette; Betty reads Marlo as a chaotic factor around the already-volatile Rick — gives way over a sustained sequence of issues to genuine, sustained friendship. By the time Marlo marries Rick (*Incredible Hulk* #418, 1994 — the famous "wedding issue"), Betty is unambiguously her closest female friend, and the friendship is reciprocal: Marlo's first instinct in crisis, repeatedly, is to call Betty.

### Dynamic

Marlo is the **only person in Betty's life who does not need anything from her**. Not interpretation of Bruce, not military intelligence on Thunderbolt, not absorption of male affect. Marlo wants Betty to come over and drink coffee and complain. The flattening relief of this on Betty is visible across the David run — she is funnier, looser, more profane in Marlo's company than she is anywhere else on the page. With Marlo she rolls her eyes at Bruce; with Bruce she never does.

Marlo's gift is **non-interpretation**. She does not psychologize Betty. She accepts the surface report and meets it. When Betty says she is fine, Marlo says "okay" and changes the subject — which paradoxically makes Betty volunteer more than she ever does with people who probe. The David-era scenes of the two of them at kitchen tables, in motel rooms during the Pantheon arc, in waiting rooms during medical events, are some of the only recorded instances of Betty *initiating* disclosure.

### Evolution

- **1988–1992:** Acquaintance through Rick. Polite mutual curiosity.
- **1992–1994:** Active friendship; Betty becomes Marlo's confidante through the Marlo-Rick courtship and through Marlo's brief abduction/replacement by a Skrull-ish double in the David run's stranger arcs.
- **1994 wedding through 1997:** Peak. They are routinely in each other's homes. Betty stands up at the wedding; Marlo is the closest thing to a sister Betty has had since her mother died.
- **1997–98 (Betty's terminal illness and death):** Marlo is one of the few permitted into the hospital room. Her grief is documented and is not theatrical — she sits.
- **Post-resurrection (2008+):** The friendship is not re-established with anything like its prior intensity. Red She-Hulk does not reach for Marlo. This is itself a datum (see Failure Mode).

### Trust and Safety

The highest registered. Marlo is one of perhaps three people in canon to whom Betty has spoken about her mother's death without prompting. Marlo also knows about the miscarriage in a way Bruce arguably does not — that is, knows the *feel* of it, not just the fact. Betty does not lie to Marlo. She withholds, but she does not fabricate.

### Failure Mode

The friendship's vulnerability is **Betty's shame about its asymmetry**. Marlo gives more than she takes because Betty offers less than she has. When Betty's life intensifies — Pantheon, illness, death — she pulls back from Marlo "to protect her," which is the same protective-withdrawal pattern she runs on Bruce in reverse. The post-resurrection failure to re-establish is, on the evidence, **Betty's avoidance of being seen in the new body by the one woman who knew the old one without performance**. Marlo would not be impressed by Red She-Hulk's swagger; Marlo would ask if she was okay. Betty cannot afford the question.

### Leverage

- **For Betty:** Marlo is her single most underused regulatory resource. A weekend in Marlo's apartment would do more for her than any pharmacological intervention. She knows this and does not use it.
- **Through Betty:** Threat or harm to Marlo is one of the few non-Bruce, non-Thunderbolt levers that will move Betty to immediate, unhesitating action. Pre-Red, this would have been pleading and intervention; post-Red, it would be violence.
- **Through Marlo:** Marlo could, with one phone call, get Betty to a place she would not otherwise go. No one else alive can claim that.

---

## 2. RICK JONES

**The brother-figure. The other survivor of Bruce. The man who has been in the room for every catastrophe of her adult life.**

### History

Present from *Incredible Hulk* #1 onward — Rick is the boy whose rescue caused Bruce's exposure to the gamma bomb; Betty meets him in the same week she meets the Hulk. For sixty years of publication time and at least two decades of in-story time, Rick is the one constant non-romantic presence in the Banner orbit. He is, functionally, Betty's brother-in-Bruce: they share the same primary attachment object, the same guilt-economy around him, the same lifelong vigilance.

### Dynamic

Younger-brother register, even when Rick ages into adulthood. Betty mothers him gently, particularly in the Lee/Trimpe era — sandwiches, clean shirts, "have you eaten." Rick reciprocates with a teenage-boy chivalry that gradually matures into something more comradely. They are united by the shared knowledge that **no one else fully understands what it is to organize one's life around Bruce**. They do not need to explain to each other why they wait by the phone.

The relationship has a low ceiling but a very high floor. They are never confidants in the deep sense — Rick is too volatile and too prone to act on what he hears — but they are reliable allies. In any room where Bruce is the subject, they read each other's micro-expressions instantly.

### Evolution

- **Silver Age:** Pseudo-sibling. Rick the kid, Betty the slightly-older young woman; both orbiting Bruce.
- **Bronze Age:** Rick's own arcs (Captain Marvel, Avengers proximity, A-Bomb later) take him away; periodic returns to the Banner orbit re-include Betty.
- **David era:** They are adults together. Rick's marriage to Marlo *triangulates* the friendship — Betty inherits Marlo through Rick, and the two couples have something briefly resembling a normal social structure.
- **Post-Betty's death:** Rick is one of the loudest mourners. His grief is on the page; he blames himself in the displaced way Bruce cannot afford to.
- **Post-resurrection / A-Bomb era:** Rick becomes a gamma-mutated being himself (A-Bomb). The shared gamma-citizenship reconfigures the relationship: they are now both members of a small involuntary club. Wary mutual recognition; not full re-intimacy.

### Trust and Safety

Moderate-to-high in *capability*; moderate in *discretion*. Betty trusts Rick to act in Bruce's interest, which is functionally her interest. She does not trust him with secrets she would not want repeated to Bruce within forty-eight hours.

### Failure Mode

Rick's loyalties are **stack-ordered with Bruce on top**. If Betty's interest ever diverged sharply from Bruce's, Rick would side with Bruce, and Betty knows this. The friendship therefore operates only in the wide region where their interests align — which is most of life, but not all of it. The miscarriage period is the documented inflection: Rick is awkward, does not know what to do, defaults to checking on Bruce. Betty notes this without complaint and adjusts her expectation downward permanently.

### Leverage

- **For Betty:** Rick is the operational contact for reaching Bruce when Bruce does not want to be reached. He is the back channel.
- **Through Betty:** Anything threatening Rick activates her at the Marlo-level, possibly higher pre-Red, because of the sibling-coding and the Bruce-link.
- **Through Rick:** Information flow runs both ways; what Betty says to Rick about Bruce should be assumed in Bruce's hearing.

---

## 3. THE PANTHEON (AGAMEMNON, ATALANTA, ACHILLES, AJAX, ANDROMEDA, ULYSSES, ET AL.)

**The closest Betty has come to a chosen extended family — and the one that taught her chosen families can also be cults.**

### History

The Pantheon arc (*Incredible Hulk* #368–425, intermittently, David) places Bruce as field leader of an immortal-adjacent paramilitary clan operating from the Mount, a hidden Arizona complex. Betty lives there for an extended period, embedded in the family architecture: her own quarters, a recognized social position as "Agamemnon's field leader's wife," and quasi-aunt status to the younger Pantheon members.

### Dynamic

This is the **only sustained communal-living arrangement of Betty's adult life**. For the first time since childhood she eats meals on a schedule, in a dining hall, with the same people. There is structure, hierarchy, ritual, shared mission. For a woman whose adult life had been motel rooms and rented houses near military bases, the Mount is a kind of relief.

She develops sub-relationships:

- **Atalanta:** Mutual respect bordering on friendship; warrior-woman regards Betty's quieter competence with something like curiosity. They do not become close, but Atalanta is one of the few who treats Betty as a person rather than as Bruce's wife.
- **Ulysses:** Strategist; intellectually compatible with Betty; conversations on the page show real exchange.
- **Achilles:** Closeted gay member whose own concealment Betty intuits and respects; quiet sympathy on her part.
- **Agamemnon:** Reads Betty correctly as the *moral conscience* of Bruce, and therefore as someone to be managed. Their interactions are polite and contain low-grade manipulation she does not initially perceive.

### Evolution

- **Arrival:** Provisional, suspicious, relieved.
- **Settling:** Genuine attachment to the place and to specific members.
- **Agamemnon's betrayal (the revelation that he is a millennia-old predator who has been using the Pantheon as cover):** Betty is among those who voted to imprison him. She is shaken less by the betrayal itself than by the recognition that she had let her guard down with an authority figure for the first time since childhood and been wrong again.
- **Departure:** Eventual; the Pantheon recedes from her life as Bruce's path diverges from theirs.

### Trust and Safety

Moderate within the bond; collapsed at the institution level after Agamemnon. She retains warmth for individuals; she will never again live inside a hierarchy that asks for her trust on credit.

### Failure Mode

She over-invested in the *system*, not in the people. When the system was revealed as corrupt, the individual relationships did not survive the institutional fracture — most went dormant. She does not write letters to Atalanta. She should.

### Leverage

- **For Betty:** A small number of extremely capable individuals owe her something and would remember her name. Unused asset.
- **Through Betty:** The Pantheon experience is a *shame point*. She trusted a patriarchal authority figure and was deceived; she does not enjoy being reminded.

---

## 4. JENNIFER WALTERS (SHE-HULK)

**The mirror cousin-in-law. The one woman in the world who shares the biological condition and is healthier with it.**

### History

Jen is Bruce's cousin, transformed via emergency gamma transfusion. She enters Betty's life in the Bruce-orbit and remains intermittently present from the early 80s onward. The relationship is structurally familial-by-marriage (cousin-in-law) but operates closer to a complicated sisterhood.

### Dynamic

Jen is everything Betty was not allowed to be: sexually self-possessed, professionally successful in her own right (attorney), publicly green and unembarrassed about it, capable of being She-Hulk *and* a person simultaneously without the dissociative shatter that defines Bruce — and that will later define Betty as Red. Pre-Red, Betty's relationship to Jen contains a documented undercurrent of envy that she handles with grace. Jen, for her part, treats Betty with a slight protective deference — recognizes that Betty's life is harder than hers and respects her for surviving it.

Post-Red, the dynamic inverts and complicates. Now Betty *is* a gamma-being, and she is *not* like Jen — she is angrier, more dissociated, more like Bruce. Jen's example becomes simultaneously an inspiration and a rebuke. The few on-page Red-She-Hulk / She-Hulk interactions (*Defenders*, scattered crossovers) show Jen offering hand-extended sisterhood and Betty being unable to fully accept it.

### Evolution

- **Pre-Red:** Cordial in-law warmth with quiet envy.
- **Death/resurrection gap:** Jen mourns Betty as family.
- **Post-Red:** Mentor-offered, mentee-resistant.

### Trust and Safety

High in capacity, low in current use. Jen is one of the few people Betty *could* call without complication; Betty's pride prevents it.

### Failure Mode

Comparison-injury. Jen's relative health with the gamma condition reads to Betty as evidence of her own failure to integrate. Rather than ask Jen *how*, she avoids Jen *because*. The avoidance is the diagnostic.

### Leverage

- **For Betty:** Jen is the single best available model of gamma-integration as a livable life. The model goes untapped.
- **Through Betty:** Threats to Jen activate gamma-family loyalty; this is a real lever post-Red.

---

## 5. DOC SAMSON (DR. LEONARD SAMSON)

**The closest figure to a therapist Betty has ever had — and the one she could never fully use because he was Bruce's therapist first.**

### History

Samson enters the Hulk mythos as Bruce's clinician and friend, gamma-empowered himself, green-haired, professionally a psychiatrist with security clearances suitable for the gamma case files. He has known Betty for the entirety of the David era and beyond.

### Dynamic

Samson treats Betty as **a colleague's wife who is also evidently in need of clinical care** and navigates the conflict-of-interest with mixed success. He is warm, attentive, will sit with her in waiting rooms, will offer professionally framed observations she could either receive as friendship or as therapy. He never bills her. He is also too entangled to be her therapist properly — he is treating her husband, he is friends with her father (at points), he has his own ego-stake in being indispensable to the gamma family.

Betty uses him as **the person she can talk to about Bruce in clinical language**. With Samson she can say "dissociative" instead of "he wasn't really here last night." This intellectualized intimacy is real and is one of her healthier registers.

### Evolution

- **Early:** Suspicion. He had a brief Banner-rivalry romantic interest in her at one point (David, mid-run) which she handled by re-establishing the professional frame; Samson absorbed the redirection without lasting damage to the alliance.
- **Middle:** Stable clinical-friendship hybrid.
- **Late David / death:** Samson is present at the dying; one of the few who can describe what is happening to her medically without flinching.
- **Post-resurrection:** Samson is himself altered, killed, returned, corrupted, re-redeemed across multiple arcs; the friendship becomes intermittent and complicated. He is no longer reliably available even when she would reach for him.

### Trust and Safety

High intellectually; medium emotionally. She trusts his expertise; she does not trust him not to tell Bruce.

### Failure Mode

**The conflict of interest she never resolved.** She needed her own clinician and instead used her husband's. This is a structural failure that contributed materially to her decades of unprocessed trauma. Had she had a clinician with no Banner-loyalty, the outcome trajectory of her psychology might have been different.

### Leverage

- **For Betty:** A trained psychiatric ear who knows the entire gamma context. Vastly underused.
- **Through Betty:** Samson knows things about her interior no one else does, including pre-death disclosures about the miscarriage. Were he compromised (and he has been), this is a substantial exposure.

---

## 6. KAREN ROSS (MOTHER, DECEASED) — THE INTERNAL MENTOR

**The mentor she has functionally, in absence. The internal voice she consults.**

### History

Karen Ross died of illness during Betty's adolescence. Canon establishes her as warmer than Thunderbolt, the affective regulator of the household, the one who could have taught Betty how to be in a body and in a marriage. Her death was followed by Thunderbolt's refusal to grieve.

### Dynamic

Karen is not a person Betty knows as an adult; she is an **internal object** Betty consults. The David run includes flashbacks and implicit cues that Betty narrates aspects of her adult life to her dead mother — particularly her wedding, the pregnancy, and her dying. This is normal mourning architecture, but in Betty it is doing the load-bearing work that a living mentor would do for someone else.

### Evolution

The relationship deepens with each adult crisis. Betty at twenty consults Karen rarely; Betty at forty consults Karen constantly.

### Trust and Safety

Absolute, because Karen cannot respond and cannot disappoint. This is also its limitation.

### Failure Mode

An internalized maternal object cannot **correct** Betty. It can only confirm her existing trajectory in a warm voice. The dead mother is a mirror, not a mentor.

### Leverage

- **For Betty:** A continuous source of self-soothing and continuity-of-self.
- **Through Betty:** Karen is sacred ground. Invocation of her by a manipulator (her father has done this; Agamemnon implicitly attempted it) is incendiary.

---

## 7. AMADEUS CHO

**Late-period younger ally; the bright nephew-figure of the gamma extended family.**

### History

Cho enters Hulk-orbit during the *World War Hulk* / Greg Pak era and becomes a recurring fixture in the broader gamma circle. By the time Betty is Red She-Hulk, Cho is one of the younger competent figures in her vicinity.

### Dynamic

Cho is too young, too brilliant, and too uncomplicated to read Betty cleanly. He treats Red She-Hulk with respect and slight wariness. The relationship is collegial, not intimate. He represents one of the few figures in her post-resurrection life who *did not know the old Betty* and therefore makes no comparison.

### Evolution

Limited but steady — collegial, with the possibility of mentorship Betty does not extend.

### Trust and Safety

Operational. She would have him at her back in a fight; she would not tell him anything personal.

### Failure Mode

She defaults to treating him as a kid even when he has earned more. A typical Betty failure: under-recognizing the agency of those younger than she.

### Leverage

- **For Betty:** A high-capability operational ally with no historical baggage. The cleanest available re-start of a relationship she has.
- **Through Betty:** Minor; not deep enough yet to be a vector.

---

## 8. NAMOR (BRIEFLY) AND THE DEFENDERS COHORT

**The post-resurrection peer-group experiment. The chosen-family that wasn't.**

### History

During the Jason Aaron *Defenders* run, Red She-Hulk is rostered alongside the Silver Surfer, Doctor Strange, Iron Fist, Namor, and others. The team is unstable, sexually charged (Namor as the gravitational center of the libidinal mess), and short-lived.

### Dynamic

This is Betty's **attempt at a non-Bruce-mediated team identity**. She is one of the boys in a way she never was; she drinks with them, fights with them, sleeps with one of them (Namor, briefly, ambiguously, on the page). The cohort is the closest thing to a peer chosen-family the post-Red Betty constructs.

It fails because the cohort itself is structurally unsound. None of these figures are well; the team is a confluence of dysregulated affects, not a holding environment. Betty is using it as a counterphobic substitute for a real social structure.

### Evolution

Intense, brief, dispersed.

### Trust and Safety

Tactical-only. None of these figures would she call in private grief. Most of them would not pick up.

### Failure Mode

Performance of belonging in the absence of belonging. She is louder there than she is anywhere else, and the loudness is the symptom.

### Leverage

- **For Betty:** Network of high-power contacts. Real, if shallow.
- **Through Betty:** Reputational — what was said and done in that period is on the record and is available to anyone who wishes to embarrass the woman she is now trying to become.

---

## 9. CLAY QUARTERMAIN AND THE S.H.I.E.L.D. PERIPHERY

**The professional acquaintances of a military daughter who has lived her whole life adjacent to a security apparatus.**

### History

Quartermain (S.H.I.E.L.D. agent, recurring Hulk-supporting cast across multiple eras) is the named representative of a category: the S.H.I.E.L.D. and Army-Intelligence officers who, over decades, have processed Betty's paperwork, debriefed her after kidnappings, escorted her to and from secure locations, and known her by sight.

### Dynamic

Cordial-professional. She knows their names, their wives' names, their kids' ages. They know hers. This is the **base-brat sociability** she learned in childhood: superficial warmth with people in uniform, who may be reassigned or killed at any time.

### Evolution

The network has been continuous from her childhood through her death and into her resurrection. It is the one structure in her life that has never *not* been there.

### Trust and Safety

Low-individual, high-aggregate. No one of them is a confidant; collectively they are the connective tissue of the world she has always lived in.

### Failure Mode

She mistakes the network's continuity for safety. It is in fact *surveillance*. Her father has used it for sixty years to track her. She knows this and tolerates it; tolerating it is part


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

*Dossier section. Subject: Elizabeth "Betty" Ross. Cross-reference §§1–5 of the analytical workup. Each entry maps history, dynamics, evolution, trust/safety architecture, failure mode, and leverage — the specific levers by which each figure can move her, and the specific costs they have already extracted.*

---

## I. RIVALS

### Major Glenn Talbot — the man her father wanted her to marry

**History.** Talbot enters Desert Base in the mid-Sixties as Thunderbolt's hand-picked subordinate and hand-picked son-in-law. He is presented to Betty in exactly the form her father understands love to take: a uniformed man of correct rank, correct posture, correct deference upward and correct command downward. He is not cruel. That is the trap. Cruelty would have given Betty something to refuse; Talbot's decency made refusal feel like ingratitude. He courts her for years across the Lee/Trimpe run, present at every base function, deferentially patient through every Banner reappearance, and finally — at the nadir of her hope, with Bruce missing or monstrous and her father visibly aging into his disappointment — she accepts him (*Hulk* #124, 1970).

**Dynamics.** Talbot was Betty's first articulate experiment in *living the life she was supposed to live*. The marriage was an act of capitulation dressed as adulthood. She was not indifferent to him — there was real warmth, real gratitude, even a kind of relief at being held by a man who came home at night — but she did not desire him, and she knew it, and the knowing curdled the affection. He, for his part, knew he was second. The marriage was a slow ledger of two people each pretending the other was first choice.

**Evolution.** Talbot's capture behind the Iron Curtain, his presumed death, his return broken and paranoid, his deterioration, and his eventual death in combat with the Hulk constitute one of the most morally complicated arcs in her early life. She had wished to be free of the marriage. She got her wish in the worst possible form. Guilt about Talbot is one of the bedrock sediments of her interior; she carried it through her second marriage and almost certainly carries it still.

**Trust / safety.** With Talbot she was *physically* safer than at any other point in her adult life and *psychologically* less safe than she admitted. He never threatened her body. He continuously, gently, threatened her sense of self by being a daily reminder of the woman she had failed to become.

**Failure mode.** The marriage failed because Betty's attachment template required absence and longing as proof of love. Talbot's presence registered as wrongness. She did not betray him — she simply could not light up for him, and he watched her not light up for years.

**Leverage.** The Talbot file remains live. Any operator wishing to destabilize her need only invoke the sequence — *you used him, you wished him dead, you got what you wished* — and she will go quiet for hours. This was the first death she caused by wanting, and she has not yet been permitted to forgive herself for it.

---

### Marlo Chandler — rival turned closest friend

**History.** Initially introduced (David era, late 80s) as a romantic interest of Rick Jones and, briefly, of Joe Fixit (the gray Las Vegas Hulk), Marlo enters Betty's orbit as a sexually self-possessed, loud, working-class Vegas woman — everything Betty has been trained not to be. The triangular geometry briefly positions Marlo as a *rival*, and Betty registers her as such in the body before she registers her in the mind.

**Dynamics.** What David then does — and what is rare in the canon — is let the two women *talk*, and discover that the rivalry was a misreading. Marlo is the first adult woman in Betty's life who treats her as a peer rather than as a function (daughter, wife, victim, hostage). She is funny, blunt, sexually frank, unimpressed by the General, unimpressed by gamma, and entirely uninterested in performing the role of Bruce's Friend. With Marlo, Betty's vocabulary opens.

**Evolution.** The friendship survives Marlo's marriage to Rick, Marlo's own death and resurrection (Marlo is briefly an avatar for Death herself), and Betty's death. It is the only lateral, secure, durable bond of Betty's adult life. Post-resurrection, contact is intermittent and characteristically avoided by Betty — partly because Marlo is one of the few people who *knew* the pre-Red Betty intimately enough to register what has been lost, and Betty cannot tolerate that mirror.

**Trust / safety.** The highest in the file. Marlo is the only person who has neither threatened her body, demanded her loyalty, nor required her to interpret a man for the world.

**Failure mode.** Betty's. She lets the friendship attenuate when she is in crisis, exactly when it could help. Avoidance is not Marlo's failure; it is Betty's signature.

**Leverage.** Marlo is a positive leverage point — the single accessible bond by which a sympathetic actor could re-anchor her. She is also a danger to any hostile operator: Marlo is one of the very few people in Betty's life who *cannot* be intimidated by Thunderbolt and *cannot* be charmed by Bruce.

---

### Jennifer Walters (She-Hulk) — the woman who got it right

**History.** Bruce's cousin; transfused with his blood after a gunshot wound; permanent, integrated, legally-functional gamma alter. Pre-existing in the family system long before Betty's own transformation.

**Dynamics.** The Betty–Jen dynamic is one of structural envy never spoken aloud. Jen is what Betty might have been if the gamma had landed differently: integrated rather than dissociated, professionally individuated rather than relationally collapsed, sexually frank without it being a reaction-formation, capable of being *green* without being made monstrous. Jen wears the family curse as a profession. Betty wore it as a wound, then as a weapon, never as a craft.

**Evolution.** Pre-Red, the relationship is cordial and slightly distant; Betty admires Jen and finds it tiring to admire her. Post-Red, the relationship sharpens into something more interesting — two gamma-affected women, one stable, one not, regarding each other across a competence gap neither names. In *Defenders*-era appearances and elsewhere, the two have shared sequences that read as the closest Betty comes to a working-through of her own monstrousness, because Jen is the proof that the transformation is not the verdict.

**Trust / safety.** Moderate. Jen would not hurt her. Jen also will not soften the truth, which Betty needs and avoids.

**Failure mode.** Betty competes silently and then withdraws. The relationship has never been allowed to mature on the page because Betty cannot tolerate sustained proximity to the integrated version of her own predicament.

**Leverage.** Jen is the only person who can say *you are not the worst case of this* and have it be received as data rather than condescension. Whether Betty will let her remains the open question.

---

## II. ENEMIES

### Emil Blonsky, the Abomination — the man who killed her

**History.** A Soviet-era operator turned, by his own choice and a stolen gamma protocol, into a permanently-disfigured, permanently-stronger dark mirror of the Hulk. Unlike Bruce, Blonsky never reverts. His monstrousness is total and irreversible — and *chosen*. He blames Bruce for the fact that his transformation cannot be undone. Betty becomes the instrument of his revenge.

**Dynamics.** Blonsky's relationship to Betty is the most malignant in the file. He understands, with a clarity Bruce has rarely permitted himself, that Betty is the gravitational center of Banner's interior. He understands further that the *most exquisite* way to hurt Bruce is not to kill Betty quickly but to poison her with his own gamma-saturated blood — to use the very substance that defines Bruce, refracted through Bruce's dark twin, to kill the woman Bruce loves. The symbolism is not accidental in the text; Blonsky chooses it.

**Evolution.** Across early appearances Blonsky kidnaps and threatens her as part of the standard rotation. The escalation to murder (*Hulk* #468, David, 1998) is not impulsive — it is a calculated act of semiotic cruelty. He does not strike. He kisses, in some versions; he contaminates; he lets her die slowly, conscious, in a hospital bed, with her father at the bedside blaming her husband. Blonsky is the most ideologically articulate of her killers because he killed her with *meaning*.

**Trust / safety.** Zero. The only relationship in the file in which trust is structurally impossible; he has demonstrated the capacity and the willingness to end her, and he derived satisfaction from doing so slowly.

**Failure mode.** Not Betty's. The failure was Bruce's failure to anticipate that Blonsky understood the geometry of his love, and Thunderbolt's failure to ever provide a perimeter that did not also imprison her.

**Leverage.** Blonsky retains, indefinitely, the symbolic position of the man who killed her. Any subsequent encounter — and post-resurrection there have been several — must contend with the fact that her body remembers dying at his hand. Operationally: Blonsky's mere proximity is destabilizing. Her response will be either freeze (pre-Red residue) or transformation (Red overlay), and which one fires first cannot be predicted.

---

### The Leader (Samuel Sterns) — the intellect that owns her file

**History.** The Hulk's archnemesis of mind; a gamma-elevated intellect whose primary activity is the *understanding* of the gamma phenomenon. He has, across decades, captured Betty more times than any other adversary, and unlike Blonsky he is rarely interested in killing her. He is interested in studying her.

**Dynamics.** This is the deepest violation in the file, less visible than the Harpy or the death because it is *epistemic*. The Leader almost certainly has a more complete biophysical and behavioral file on Betty than any other entity in the Marvel universe, accumulated across multiple captivities, across the Harpy episodes (which he engineered or refined), across the Red transformation (in which the Intelligencia — which he leads — is the architect of her resurrection-as-weapon).

**Evolution.** Early arcs use her as bait. Mid-period arcs use her as substrate — she is a research object. The Loeb-era Intelligencia plot reveals that her *return from death* and her *transformation into Red She-Hulk* were not gifts; they were procedures. She did not consent to her own resurrection.

**Trust / safety.** Zero, and uniquely chilling because the Leader does not need proximity to harm her. He needs only knowledge, which he has.

**Failure mode.** Betty has never fully processed the fact that her second life is, in significant part, the Leader's lab note. She avoids the implication because the implication makes her existence itself feel curated.

**Leverage.** Devastating, if invoked precisely. *You exist because he allowed it* is the sentence that would put her on the floor. Conversely: if she ever decides to confront the Intelligencia *for* this, rather than around it, she would do so with a focus and a violence currently latent in her.

---

### M.O.D.O.K. — the architect of the Harpy

**History.** Mental Organism Designed Only for Killing; AIM's gamma-engineered command-intellect; recurring orchestrator of Hulk-adjacent torments. M.O.D.O.K. is the technical author of the first Harpy transformation (*Hulk* #168, 1973, via the Bi-Beast plot in collaboration with the broader AIM apparatus), the operation by which Betty's body was first rewritten without her consent into a form that screamed.

**Dynamics.** What M.O.D.O.K. did to her is, in the long psychological view, more important than what Blonsky did. Blonsky killed her body once. M.O.D.O.K. taught her body that it could be replaced. The Harpy episode established the somatic precedent for every subsequent transformation — Modok captivities, the Loeb resurrection, the Red shift. Her body learned, under his hand, that it was a substrate.

**Evolution.** Subsequent encounters reinforce rather than introduce trauma. By the post-Red period, Betty's relationship to her own form is so plastic, so accustomed to being overwritten, that M.O.D.O.K.'s threats register as *familiar* rather than novel — which is its own kind of horror.

**Trust / safety.** Zero. Unique among her enemies in that M.O.D.O.K. targets her *interiority* (via the body) rather than her life. He has never tried to kill her. He has only tried to author her.

**Failure mode.** Betty's failure with M.O.D.O.K. is repetition: she has not yet found a way to refuse the substrate-role except by becoming a different kind of substrate (Red). True refusal would require a Betty who is content to be only herself — a Betty the narrative has not yet produced.

**Leverage.** Anything that returns her to the Harpy memory specifically. The Harpy is the wound she has the least vocabulary for, because it predates her vocabulary.

---

### The Gargoyle, the Sandman, Tyrannus, Boomerang, the Missing Link, et al. — the rotation

**History.** Across the Lee/Trimpe Silver Age and well into the Bronze, Betty is the abductable. Kidnapped by the Gargoyle in the inaugural arc; carried off by Tyrannus into Subterranea; menaced by the Sandman, the Missing Link, Boomerang's crews, and a long bench of secondary actors who needed a hostage and reached for the nearest available daughter.

**Dynamics.** These are not, individually, formative. Collectively they are the *atmosphere* of her early adult life: she lived under a permanent low pressure of being-taken. The cumulative effect — never staged on the page but unavoidable in inference — is a baseline hypervigilance she has trained herself to mask. She does not flinch easily. She has stopped registering small alarms because the medium-sized alarms have been constant.

**Trust / safety.** N/A individually; collectively zero, in the sense that *the world* is the threat actor, not any one of them.

**Failure mode.** The chronic exposure produced the high-tolerance, sudden-break pattern that defines her stress response. She absorbs and absorbs and then one day is the Harpy.

**Leverage.** Any orchestrated abduction in the *style* of the Silver Age — disguised men, sedative, transport, a desert holding cell — will trip a deep procedural memory. She will not panic. She will go very still and very competent, in the way of a person who has been here before and knows the timetable.

---

### The Intelligencia (collective: the Leader, M.O.D.O.K., the Wizard, the Mad Thinker, Red Ghost) — the cartel that owns her second life

**History.** A consortium of the Marvel intellect-villains; assembled most coherently in the Loeb Hulk run; the operational engine behind the Red Hulk and Red She-Hulk programs.
